=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
In Sherlock Holmes Consulting Detective, you are presented with a mystery to solve, and it is then up to you to trace the threads of evidence through the byways and mansions of nineteenth century London. You will interview suspects, search the newspapers for clues, and put together the facts to reach a solution.

Why were two lions murdered in Hyde Park? Who is responsible for the missing paintings from the National Gallery? Who murdered Oswald Mason and why? These are just a few of the cases that will challenge your ingenuity and deductive abilities.

This is not a board game: No dice, no luck, but a challenge to your mental ability. The game has been thoroughly researched for Holmesian and Victorian accuracy so as to capture a feeling of that bygone era.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
A unique game where you begin with a scenario and then choose a location where you interact with a suspect or a location.  The information enables you to move to another location to get more information.  If you are able to solve the cases with the least amount of information, your score goes up.  

The early scenarios have problems where you can solve the major case, but the leaps in logic that Holmes makes defy believability.  The third or fourth case may be broken but after that I have read the cases improve.  If you have a chance to play I recommend the game for its uniqueness.

RATING: 9.5
This is a really unique game.  I really love playing it.  It has more of the feel of solving a puzzle or mystery rather than a traditional competition. The game is great as either solitaire or even as a group effort.  The game rewards and requires players to use true deductive and inductive reasoning and general logic.  A remarkable accomplishment for a game.  There are no dice, card draws or other luck factors.  This adds to the feeling of accomplishment.  There are 10 scenarios in the game.  It takes time to complete them so the game does have value for the price, but may have limited replay-ability.  Maybe new scenarios will be available in the future.  Still a great and unique game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I love everything Sherlock Holmes. Basil Rathbone was always the one for me in the films and bookwise I always resort to the 3 volume New Annotated Sherlock Holmes put together by Leslie S Klinger. So when I got this last Christmas I dove straight into it.

I loved it. Then liked it. Then after completing all ten cases was umming and ahhing about my feelings for it. Sherlock your a cunning example of the human mind. Your leaps of deduction are far beyond my means of human thought or logic. I found this dispiriting and in a few cases could just not see how he could make the leaps of deduction he made to solve cases with the information he would have had at hand if he had followed the leads stated in the solutions. Dare I say it ... Holmes came across by the end of the game as a slightly sneaky cheat ... and one that rubbed my face in it!

I enjoyed the game and it gave me some great gaming moments. I remember losing myself down the consulting detective rabbit hole accompanied by a bottle of very nice red on a 7 hour train journey. Who did what during that investigation I cannot remember.

And I never will as I sold it after I completed all 10 cases. As much as I have hesitancy in my praise for the game in this mini review, If I could I would crack it all open again fresh faced hoping in some way to match Sherlock Holmes powers of deduction .... just to let him laugh at me all over again. It is that good .. and then that frustrating.

RATING: 7
This is a game wholly unlike anything else I've played to date, and I love it. Yes, it can only be played 10 times unless you wait a looooong time between replays, but based on my first play those 10 times are going to be a lot of fun. I like deduction games in general, and this one puts a pure and thematic spin on that genre.

Our first game was as a group of six playing as one team, and while we did horribly score-wise (a whopping 10 points...) we had a blast getting there. I'm not sure I'd enjoy it playing as individuals, but as a team game it's a hoot. Holmes makes for a very challenging opponent, and what he learns and how he learns it is instructive in terms of how the game thinks (and thinks you should think).

The components are fantastic, and do a great job of conveying the atmosphere of Holmes' London. The only area where they fall down is typos: We ran into quite a few in just one game, presumably an artifact of the translation process coupled with sloppy proofreading. (And the game does have a shitload of text.)

RATING: 7
Definitely a niche game, unlike any other i've played. It's not eye catching or exciting to look at per-say. The fun comes from the story and its theme. Despite being a text based game that relies almost totally on mentally generated excitement rather than visual elements, it's quite immersive in gameplay. It's a total sandbox game where you can go from clue to clue or from person to person as you please, solving the crime on your own time. It's exciting to figure it out on your own, without being led by the hand. You could possibly not even solve the case correctly; and you sure won't ever beat sherlock's score. i don't know if you can even call this a game, it's more like a mental exercise using some text and your imagination. Hardly what i'm used to playing. But that being said, it was a very unique and enjoyable experience.

RATING: 3
This was supposed to be a great game! It involves deduction, which I find interesting. The pieces look really nice. Its open ended, allowing you to travel around London and question who you want about the cases. You even get to interact with Sherlock Holmes himself! Fascinating stuff!

Then I played it. The more people you talk to about the case the more questions and doubt arise in your mind. Everywhere you go leads you down seemingly endless paths of vague possibility. People you think may be key characters to question have little to say, people who you’d never think have anything of use to offer wind up being incredibly important. I’d spend two hours pouring over the fine details of a case only to have no better idea of “who done it” than when I first opened the case file.

It’s impossible to get any kind of decent score, forget win a case (which involves getting a better score than Sherlock himself). This makes the game frustrating at first, then boring, then pointless.

RATING: 7
Finally, after owning it for years I got to try out a case with my girlfriend.  

After exhausting every lead possible we arranged our accusation and got it right.  I am sure Sherlock would have had the answer in 5 turns or less...

An amazingly immersive game which can be played competitively or co-operatively.

I love deduction games although I usually make critical errors when it comes to Code 7 or Black Vienna.

It was nice to see us get the murder right for a change!  Many people say Pandemic was the first co-operative game with merit.  I dis-agree with that assessment now.  It was SH: Consulting Detective.

I find it really interesting that this game won the SdJ.  I think this would be difficult to play with pre-teen children.  This game just seems so different than any other picks (either before or after).  

With the expansions I could see this easily become addicting for a long, long time.

RATING: N/A
Still in Shrink Wrap - Unopened
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[microbadge=1714]

[u]30-May-2016[/u]:  To put it briefly, this "game" puts you right in the heart of a Sherlock Holmes mystery, allowing you to make the decisions of where the story goes, what clues are important, and finding the answer to the mystery at hand. I put the word game in quotes, because I think this is more of an interactive story than a typical hobby game we normally play. The rules offer you a way to tally points at the end and compare your results to Sherlock himself as a win condition. But if you play that way, I would guess your experience will be frustrating and disappointing. Rather, Consulting Detective is more an immersive experience than a game you are trying to win.

This might be the most thematic game in my collection. You really are the one trying to solve the mystery. The designer and publisher have done an impressive job, overall, putting this together. It's all text on paper. There are no fancy cardboard bits to move around. No minis. No cards, even. It's a box full of papers. And lots and lots of words. There are ten case books, ten "newspapers," a map of London, and a directory of names and places to visit. You'll need to provide some paper and a good pencil, because you need to take notes, probably the more the better. Each casebook opens with the setup of the mystery written just like a "real" Sherlock Holmes story. This person was killed here, with this type of weapon. He worked over there. Is/was married to this person. And this other person maybe witnessed part of the crime. Now, go!

The difficulty, of course, is putting all of the pieces together. There is most certainly a high level of deduction involved when trying to come to a conclusion about the crime and its perpetrators. Some places you visit and people you speak to will give you several pieces of information. Other times, it will be an extremely brief encounter without any help offered to your case. You'll need to track this information, decide which pieces fit into the puzzle you are putting together, and then make a resolution based on your deduction skills.

There are some component issues as well. The font in the case books is awful, in my opinion. It's all italicized and "fancy." The line width is too thin to stand out on the parchment paper stylized background. Some of the letters were unintelligible to me, and I had difficulty reading the story, let alone take notes and make deductions based on what I had read. I got used to it, over the three cases, but it could be much, much better.  And the typos, oh my! They are all over the place. At first, I didn't notice many. But once I did, I couldn't help but see them again and again. As elaborately intertwined the as the story is, you would think the editing would have been equally impressive. Perhaps it was mainly due to translation from one language to another, but it detracts from what is a creative puzzle of disjointed paragraphs.

But to wrap up...Wow! What a highly thematic, immersive experience Consulting Detective provides. Not only are you involved with the story as it progresses, you are in it, making the decisions as to what is important to note and what direction to head next. It's quite impressive how this thing is all put together, with the basic setup of a story, the long and winding puzzle of the narrative, and the intertwining of the newpapers and map of London with the case being solved. Yes, there are both moments of frustration and success when it comes to your own mastery of deduction. But the good thing is, you can go back and look for those juicy tidbits of information that you missed the first time, and see just how it all comes together in the end.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
I'm normally not a fan of cooperative games but my non-gaming wife is at least a fan of Sherlock Holmes so I bought this game, hoping that we'd both like this narrative activity

But is Sherlock Holmes Consulting Detective a game or an activity? There are no playing pieces, no board you move around on, and no cards to draw and play. There is a booklet for each of the ten adventures, telling you what happens at certain London locations, but is this really different from the old (and pretty boring) choose your own adventure books?

As a matter of fact, yes. Instead of a few limited choices, you have a London map at your disposal and are free to go anywhere for clues. Additional clues, as well as a sense of authenticity, is provided by newspapers, one per case. The clues themselves are cleverly written, telling several intervowen intrigues, that may or may not be related to the main case. The actual "game" is about revealing the murderer and motif, using as few clues as possible, but the game experience come from participating in a detective story that feels like it was written by Sir Arthur Conan Doyle himself.

Sherlock Holmes Consulting Detective may not be a game for everybody and even then it will only last for ten sessions. However, it does offer a pleasant experience and is one of few games that my wife appreciates.

RATING: 9.98
The best solo/couple and deduction game in existence.  Shut Up and Sit Down called it the best game for couples and listed it the #5 board game of all time.  Since their video review, the game (1981 release originally, mind you) has shot up from barely in the top 200 to now in the top 70, and that is despite it being out of print for half a year or so.

I have only played the first 2 missions, and I have heard there is a big flaw with the 3rd one, but honestly, I have gotten more than $27 or the $40 it might be going for out of it.  It's that good.  It's so clever, makes you feel stupid, yet makes you want to keep trying to beat Sherlock.

At the same time, I know there are only 10 missions (still wondering if the expansions that came out in the 80s will see a re-release now), so I am hesitant to continue playing with my gf because it's like a great book: once you start a mission you can't stop but you are very sad when it's over.  Brilliant.  It's as intelligent as the BBC Sherlock (disclaimer: I have only seen the first 3) and really a game every single gamer should try.

Just buy it.

RATING: 8
[b]Sherlock Holmes Consulting Detective[/b]

Case #1
Enjoyed our first case (2 players)! Your gray cells are put to the challenge, though at least for the first case, I can say it was solved without too much of a problem. 

The greatest problem (and enjoyment!) is in that you are faced with so many possible avenues to explore. You must pick and choose, prioritize to be most efficient and solve the mystery in the least amount of steps, trying to outdo Sherlock Holmes.

The map, the public directory, and daily newspaper are excellent tools, and all three must be used. There is a fair amount of reading, thinking, re-reading, discussing, and sometimes arguing with your team mate(s), ie. the impatient and gut-instincted are pitted against the more patient and analytical.

Currently wondering how this is played in teams? 2v2v2v2? Are all the indices shared publicly?

Shutup and Sitdown review:
[video=51150][/video]


Case #2 (May 1, 2016)
5 players (my gf invited her girl friends, the original idea was to play my new AEG edition of Junta with 6, but one friend had to leave after dinner, so we went with Sherlock). Decided it was best not to split into teams, maybe some other time. The three were new to the game, so they first played Case #1, and enjoyed the game so much we continued with #2. This mystery was more difficult: we thought we had it solved, but were very surprised to learn we had gotten only 50% correct. Hmmm! The game worked very well. No alpha leader problems. Everyone had a very fun time.

RATING: 9
Owned the original Sleuth boxed edition as a kid, and while the nostalgia factor does come into play, this is still a wonderful, thematic game that's unlike anything else in my collection.

Seems pointless to try to beat Holmes - and I've never used the scoring system.  The joy of the game comes from immersing oneself in the experience and taking one's time in working through the cases.  Not something to be rushed...in fact, with only ten cases, and no real replayability beyond waiting 20 years for the details of the cases to be forgotten, it's an experience to be savored.

Some translation issues from the French and font/hand-written notes can be difficult to read.

RATING: 6.5
It's like a package of 10 murder mystery dinner kits where all the parts have textual stand ins. So no dress-up required.

But: 1) is this your sort of thing? 2) is it a board game or a murder mystery dinner? (the rating I've given is for SHCD as a board game, but as a murder mystery dinner I'd rate it a 10)

The game is almost unlosable, adjudged from the point of view of correctly solving the provided questions. All you have to do is follow enough leads. (Contrast this to Witness, which features 64 delightful mysteries in one, but is very much losable due to having an actual game mechanic) There is a rubric that subtracts points for not being efficient, which helpfully encourages the obsessives and perfectionists to pore and re-pore over provided data, engage in long, drawn-out discussions over which lead to visit next, and in general does what it can to revere the god of irony.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
A game about detectives can't get truer to life than this -the only hint of structure being that each player gets one clue at a time from the book. Apart from that, the full wealth of materials available (directory, newspaper extracts, map, case book) can be used at any time to solve the pending case. There's the obvious tension of working against the clock -two hours pass by very quickly. 

Of course the players will never get the solution as quickly as Sherlock Holmes, but if you skip the complete answers the first time a couple of replays per case can still be available for the player's entertainment. Unfortunately this can't be repeated over and over again -the only real problem in an otherwise impeccable game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Gioco molto interessante per la sua originalità. Si tratta quasi di un gioco di ruolo dove i giocatori, collaborando, dovranno risolvere un caso cercando di far meglio dello stesso Holems, o per lo meno avvicinarsi a lui, cercando di risolvere il caso senza perdere troppo tempo in piste false. La bellezza del gioco sta nella libertà assoluta di azioni dei giocatori che a loro discrezione potranno seguire le piste che riterranno opportuno, far visita a sospettati o esperti in varie materie e trarre informazioni utili anche dai quotidiani. Per risolvere il caso spesso serve intuito, attenzione, fortuna e una buona analisi. La riuscita delle partite per il divertimento di tutti riesce tanto più il gruppo si conosce ed è compatto. Il gioco ha il difetto di non essere rigiocabile, ovvero una volta fatte le 10 avventure il tutto è finito, salvo trovare nuove avventure che immagini spesso sarà possibile scaricare. Personalmente penso che il gioco poteva essere integrato con un cd con le tracce di tutte le letture.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
A deduction game quite unlike any other - almost an RPG or an extended piece of interactive literature. It's deeply thematic, quirky, beautifully written, rich with possibilities and historical touches, and I absolutely love it. But then, I'm the sort of odd person whose favourite bit of any spy thriller is the endless poking about in dusty libraries looking for clues.

It's virtually a given that you need the right crowd for this - invariably a group of Holmes buffs who will dress up and act their way throughout the thing. And it's peculiarly interesting how collaboration arises from the mechanics and storytelling themselves, even if you start off with purely selfish intentions.

RATING: 9
I love this. Being a fan of the old Sherlock Holmes and Agatha Cristie Stories this is exactly my "cup of tea". This has a lot of what I wanted from Time Stories but only got to a small extent from that game so far. I would love it though if parts of the Time Stories mechanic could be implemented in future cases (as there seem to be new cases coming now) and that is specifically the idea of tokens that mark what knowledge you already have and that might unlock new options at certain locations. 

So far played: 
Case 1: astonishing 90 or 65 points (1 of the main questions we completly guessed, but did so surprisingly correct, so we are not sure how much we deserve those extra 25 points)

Case 2: we were sure we solved the main case at some point but then wanted to follow up some more stuff so we calculated scores for both (that turned out to be not too different): we solved the main case with 85 points and after several more leads with some extra things figured out we had 80 points)

Case 3: a bit of an odd (and weaker) one for us. Officially we scored 40, but we basically had all the information we "used" at the end, already 7 completly useless leads earlier as we were almost desperatly trying to find a more concrete hint for something we asumed but couldn't really confirm. Turned out (looking back through the case book after the solution) that that proof in some form never really existed, so it just felt like a bit of a leap for us.

Case 4: 70 points and in contrast to the last we felt pretty confident on this one, we did spend quiet some time with the "sidequest" though (and it even felt like we solved another murder that noone ever asked us about :( )

Case 5: First time we officially beat Holmes with 110 points. Liked this case a lot, the crypted message and also one of the other notes were an interesting puzzle and overall the pieces came together nicely in this one.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is one of my favorite games.  I love the fact that you can play alone, with one other person, or with a group.  The components are of good quality and include a map of London, newspaper archives (printed in book form, but still nice), a directory of London residents and businesses, and introductory materials that set the scene (in case you're not a Holmes' fan) and establish how you are to proceed in order to solve each case.  The cases themselves are plausible and the clues are reasonable.  It's possible to draw this game out for hours if your group is given to debate, or to wrap things up relatively quickly if all players are in agreement as to how best to proceed, and on the interpretation of the clues collected.  

As much as I love this game, it has its faults. For instance, part of the scoring for each case is based on a quiz requiring knowledge of Conan Doyle's Sherlock Holmes stories.  This puts those not in the know at a disadvantage. Furthermore, being penalized for having needed to visit more locations than Holmes supposedly did in search of clues is annoying. My group found that in almost every case, if we had only visited the places Holmes did, we would not have had sufficient information to solve the case. And then there are the typos--no excuse for that.    (5+5) solo plays: 2

RATING: 8.5
If you're tired of victory tracks and farming, this is a fantastic experience for any number of people. While we can quibble about the exact classification of Sherlock Holmes: Consulting Detective as a game, that won't distract from how immersive, atmospheric and beautiful the game really is. I would suggest treating the game as a standard RPG campaign with your friends. Thematically, the flow of game is simple: find clues, chase down suspects, and put it altogether for the next step in the case. But be forewarned: Do NOT try and beat Sherlock. His strategy is the fewest leads, giving you only half the story. If you want the full immersive experience, go for the red herrings and obvious false leads of the case. The most successful strategies I've seen have been to focus on characters and locations that will give you the MOST information.  The game will throw red herrings and a slew of unnecessary information, slyly tempting you to chase down every lead and fit every clue within the giant puzzle of the case but that will take you further from Sherlock's path! The game doesn't want you to exhaust every resource, but to be efficient and use the knowledge you already have to the best of your ability. The cooperative experience was amazing and filled with invigorating debate and discussion (like Mysterium). Powers of reasoning and observation, everything that Sherlock Holmes is the standing epitome of, it's all here in this fantastic game. And there's expansion cases coming later in the year!

RATING: 6
This is more for solitaire or group co-operative play if you're into that kind of thing. Pick the most likely spot for the most revealing clues, read the text associated with the spot, then pick the next most likely spot, and so on. So it's more like reading a novel than it is playing a game. If you want to score as well as "Sherlock" does in the answer section, simply hope that you prioritised the same spots as he and picked up all the same nuances. For competitive play, you run into the problem of having to pass the clue book around, and some of the text is lengthy, therefore a lot of downtime between turns.

RATING: 7
"Excellent mystery-solving co-op. Rich and thematic, but not in an overwhelming way. However, the first case was more fun than the next three. Hopefully they'll get better again."

What I like (based on 1 case so far):
- Thematic and logical cases with lots of flavor
- Each case can be completed in 60-90 minutes
- Avoids the bloat and confusion of Gumshoe (a later game by the same folks)

What I don't like:
- The number of clues needed by Holmes are silly (low)
- "Only" 10 cases in the box (obviously more would be nice)
- The first case was great. The second was OK. The third and fourth were disappointing. Hopefully they'll get better again. 

This is an excellent mystery-solving game. It's very pure, where you read the introduction to the case, and then choose where to go or who to talk to in order to gain clues. The clues make thematic sense (unlike some games which rely on logic puzzles).

I would put the weight around 2.0, much lower than BGG voters. I guess I don't count the immersive depth as "weight".

RATING: 9
I was sceptical about this one, because I read some bad reviews from people I respect.

I bought it because my wife likes mystery literature and because she is pregnant and it's difficult for her to lean over the table, to play typical board games.

Worked like a charm. Like a good book. Like a great movie. Plus we have all the time we wan't to chat with each other about the outcome. Really felt we were solving the cases ourselves.

Last week we went to a concert, and took a case with us to solve while we were waiting for the show to start. You can practicaly play it anywhere and without the need for every one to be round the table. I strongly believe this game shines as a co-op. You shouldn't play seperatly. 

Just go with the flow. I make notes on the clues I get. I make family trees. I colour the places I visited on a copy of the map. Real detectives!

Great, Great, Great!!!

I agree that there is no replayability. In that sense I don't consider it a board game, but a beautiful experience that worths every penny spent!

Just don't rush. Take your time to study the clues you got and put them together after every clue. It's more rewarding to solve a case witn less clues, than reading your way through the case.

(Wife's Point of View: She plays witn me EVERY time I ask her. With usual board games she plays with me 2 times, if I ask her ten times. Do the maths and purchase)

RATING: 8
The new version as pictured, not the 1981 original.

Have been meaning to get this to the table for months, but wanted to be able to do it justice as I've been relishing it.

OK, I played the first of the ten cases... The Munitions Magnate.

I don't want to spoil the case for anyone because there is unlikely to be any replayability in the cases, but you know that when you buy it.

Woohoo... I correctly deduced the perpetrator and the motive, but didn't get all the nuances of the case, so I scored 85 out of the possible 140 points.

It was my first case and I was trying to be meticulous and followed 15 scorable leads compared to Sherlock's miserly 4 !

That meant I had to deduct 55 points, leaving a final total of only 30.

But still a moral victory :D

:thumbsup:  Uber thematic

:thumbsup: Beautifully produced. High quality documents and love the Newspaper sheets for each case.

:thumbsdown: No real replayability, but well worth the purchase price for 10 great cases to solve.

RATING: 6
In Sherlock Holmes: Consulting Detective, players attempt to solve a mystery by visiting various witnesses and other people connected to the case and listening for clues in the stories they tell.  Each turn, a player decides who to visit, including a number of always-available people like the coroner or Lestrade at Scotland Yard, or they can visit someone who's specifically connected to this case (including people learned about over the course of the investigation).  Players are encourages to compete with Holmes by trying to solve the case visiting as few witnesses as Holmes does, but that's more of an aspirational goal.  When the players are ready to make an accusation, they must answer a series of questions related to the case and can then compare their answers to Holmes.  This game involves a lot of frantic unstructured note-taking, as you never know which details might be important later, and you can't really review the text of old visits.  I didn't hate it, but I thought it was more work than it was worth.

(1 play)

RATING: 8
Cooperative sleuthing. Players are presented with a case, and attempt to solve it by gathering clues from the days newspaper, reading descriptions of the crime scene or interviews with suspects/bystanders, consulting the London Directory, and a large dash of Holmsian deduction. The cases are well-written and seem quite difficult. I think the game achieves its purpose perfectly, so if it sounds fun, you'll probably like it. The only major flaw is that I can't imagine there's much replay value. Expansions to the rescue!

RATING: 9
This isn't a game, it's an experience. I bought this in the hope my son (in his 20's) would enjoy it when he stayed over for a few days. It was amazing!! We played 5 cases over the weekend and spent hours discussing it, figuring them out, chasing leads.  He loved it that much he made me promise not to play until I see him again . So now I've got a 2 month wait on my hands!!  
There are definitely some cases better than others but my advice is just enjoy the whole experience without even caring to try and beat Holmes. He solves cases in minimal leads so if you managed to beat him then you would have more than likely made a few good guesses, and if you're wrong then you'll just feel disappointed and you won't be able to go back because you now know the solution,  and yes you might get there quickly but you'll just miss out on the pleasure of really figuring it out and following it through completely.  The sense of satisfaction at the conclusion is something to savour. It's a great feeling when you get stuck, to brainstorm , and have a breakthrough. And trust me, that will happen. Remember to use newspapers from previous cases, not just the one from the same date.   
My games of choice are usually takes like Mangrovia, Helios, Catan, Marco Polo Splendor, Pandemic , Sushi Go, Love Letter,  but this  blew me away completely.  It's worth every penny and it will hopefully bring you the same joy. Once me and my son finally complete this then I think I might actually grieve!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
One of them readin' games. Read the case background, then the newspaper, then the clues. And be sure to take notes. Not for everyone. I've only played solo. With more it might be more fun, but how to handle all that reading? 

The cases are tough. Are they fair? The scoring probably isn't. Holmes himself uses the fewest clues possible, but for us, there is usually no clear next best step (more like 2-3 equally good ideas). So I don't think you should take seriously slight differences in scores. In fact, maybe you shouldn't worry about your score at all. The game wouldn't be worth it if you solved every case in five clues.

RATING: 10
I've been 'addicted' to this hobby/pastime for nearly 20 years, due to making the decision (after rolling for a character sheet in a RPG intro) to fill the rest of the evening playing board/card games in the next room. My heart was set to learn RPG as I like the imaginative stimulation, but I wasn't confident that I had the mental resources to pull off an entertaining narrative. So into the foreign world of Siedler and Entwicklung I went, admittedly confused, without the need to be embarrassed within a prepared fantasy world. But 6Nimmt was the highlight for the evening and one of the first games I introduced to the family I married into when I migrated to Australia. The fun we had made me look into more games using Sumo/Games Cabinet for information leading to the next 'hit'. 

Spiel des Jahres was becoming part of my vocab, hence my interest in Sherlock to replace the burnout with Cluedo. But I was too interested now in the mechanism heavy designs of the Eurogame and donated my Sleuth copy to my overseas mum, without putting pen to paper.

Fast forward 15 years or so, worn out by mechanisms, resource conversion etc, marvelling at how my boys develop, especially in the acquisition of language (I've tried to learn Deutsch), I've taken a stronger interest in narrative. In summary I'm no longer fascinated by what goes on under the hood of the eurogame engine, how to squeeze as much potential and forward movement on the score track by doing the maths of input vs output; I just want to simply get in, go for a drive, see what scenery I will encounter as this makes for far more interesting, emotional memories than relating tales of drag race meets. I was now ready for the first Ystari edition of Sherlock. I didn't anticipate it would become the best game I've played so far, knocking Siedler from its 10 rating.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A simply amazing game. A collection of paragraphs, newspapers, maps, and clues that give you an interesting story to try and solve. 

Decide who you want to talk to and look him to find out what he knows. Figure it all out and compare your score to Sherlock's.

The only problem with this game is that the replayability can suffer once you know whodunnit. I've found that "GMing" the game for others can be a great way to experience the same story again. If more people buy this, we may see expansions as well.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
It's not a game. But it's one of the most enchanting experiences I had with friends who play boardgames.

You know, my biggest problem with board gaming in general is that sometimes you meet old friends and decide to play a boardgame, but for the duration of the game, they stop being people and become simply "players", so after hours of gaming you still haven't talked to them about how they are doing and what they're up to, because, frankly, you are playing a game and only talking and acting within its boundaries.

This is different, because you control the pace, there is no "turn" or "phase" you have to follow. Suddenly, it's not a distraction, but part of the game to leave the table in order to make coffee or go for a walk together (or seperately). And inbetween (at least for us), we naturally started talking about ourselves and caught up on things without anybody going "guys, can we please focus on the game?? It's your turn, what do you do?". 

In short, it's unique, go and play it, it will enrich your life.

RATING: 8
Been a long time since I've played this but I've always enjoyed it.  It might even be so long that I can play it all again and not remember the solutions. 

Although It's always good to see if you can match Holmes' rating, in reality it's virtually impossible as there are so many locations to visit and what seems the logical line of inquiry was circumvented by Holmes' super sense that enables him to solve the case in the barest minimum of clues. In any case, you'll often want to visit 'just one more location' to flesh out your case and perhaps give you a fighting chance to answer some of the peripheral questions at the denouement. 

A slight annoyance, as far as I can remember, is that in a murder case you would expect that a visit to the coroner or police surgeon would always yield results. But, presumably to add a bit of variety to the cases, this isn't always as rewarding as you would hope.

I have also played the Mansion House and Queen's Park modules. The QP module is in fact one huge case; a familiarity with storyline of 'The Maltese Falcon' may be a help here (or even a hindrance).

RATING: 7
I was really skeptical about this game, but boy did we enjoy it. The idea is this, you are Sherlock's minions. You are presented with a mystery. You are given a scenario book that contains all the dialogue of everyone you can talk to about the case in the city. You also get a map of London and essentially a yellow pages for the map. The goal is to solve the mystery in as few interviews as possible. Players choose when to end the game, and when they do, Holmes as of course already solved the mystery and you are scored based on your ability to answer 6 questions about the mystery correctly. Then you lose points for each person you interviewed in excess of who Holmes's process through the case. We really had a ball, and I'm only lamenting that there aren't an infinite number of mysteries in the box. With only 9 more mysteries to go, I won't run out soon, but knowing there's an end brings me some lamentation.

I will say, the new printing of the game we have does have several glaring and frustrating translation errors. The player aid on the back of the book uses German(?) style direction abbreviations in place of NW, NE, SW, SE and we found a "die" in place of "the", and one or two other little errors that threw us off a little.

RATING: 4
I wouldn't call SHCD a board game, but an activity, which makes it really hard to rate. Ultimately, when I want to play a board game, this type of experience is not what I'm after. Even as an activity, I found it frustrating that we wanted to go places (which turned out to be vitally important) that we couldn't find on the miniscule map. While reading the solution, none of us could figure out how Sherlock cracked the case with the provided information—he seemed to make huge logical leaps rather than relying on solid evidence. I was also annoyed that the culprit turned out to be one of the most likely suspects, who we had ruled out as too absurdly obvious. In the end, I'd rather read a good mystery novel which would take less time and effort for a greater payoff.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
An immersive deduction game where you are working with Sherlock Holmes and are presented with a case that needs solving. The game is very thematic and captures the atmosphere of the era well. There is no hand holding as you are given a short story of how the case came to your (or Holmes') attention along with a directory and map of London with the day's newspaper. That is it, now venture forth and solve the case by visiting locations in London and interviewing persons of interest.

If played competitively, the player who solves the case with the least amount of visits/interviews wins. But I have found it more fun for everyone to play cooperatively as we all just sit around the table spitballing ideas about what our next move should be. Once you know the solution, there is no point in playing again which limits the replayability. There are ten cases included with the base game so it will last a decent amount of time before needing more cases. More of a role playing game, this crime mystery game excels at storytelling and is always thoroughly enjoyed.

RATING: 2
This is more of an activity than a game.  It is very story driven, and you spend most of your time reading.

I would like to point out that there isn't really any deduction in the game.  It's all inference - just like all the movies/tv adaptations of Sherlock Holmes.

You will not spend your time making deductions.  You spend your time trying to gather as much information to build a theory for motive and opportunity - no deduction involved.

My wife and I very much disliked this game.  It's so boring.  We weren't engaged in the stories.  We didn't like the confusing nature of the language (era).  The print is hard to read.  It's just a bad experience.

RATING: 9
I started a solo game of this and instantly decided it would be so much more fun with others. This was also an introduction to know how it all works and will make future cases easier. I can't wait to do another one.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The Ystari/Asmodee edition has its flaws, but the concept still works, saving the game from its shoddy production. Overwrought fonts, glossy paper (perfect for not reading in times of lamplit evening gameplay), ridiculous typos clearly from lack of editing beyond spellcheck, etc. mar the game. People, for example, go for "carnage rides," an error that can't be explained by keyboard error. "Who might have wanted to kilt him," is asked of an obviously most unfortunate victim. 

The map of London is pretty good, although the numbering system seems screwy, and makes finding places take unneeded time. The writing and names of some of the incidental characters can produce guffaws, but the whole thing maintains a goofy charm while retaining enough of the feel of Holmes and Watson to make for an enjoyable evening.

Upon reflection, it's more fun playing the game in non-scoring mode, with everyone pitching in to soak up as much of the knowledge and atmosphere as possible, rather than rush to conclusions in order to try to beat Holmes.

UPDATE Aug-6-2009:
My parents apparently discarded this game from their basement, so when an opportunity came to reacquire it through trade I took it.

This is a paragraph-driven game, but unlike other paragraph-based games, this has zero replayability. Once you solve a case if you're lucky you'll forget all about it, because that's the only way you'll play that case again. Other games at least have a roleplaying aspect, and a larger number of paragraphs to reference. So -- it is what it is. Still, the prose is very well done, in the style of Sir Doyle. Your characters don't always ask the questions you're looking for, but that in itself is a clue you can use to solve the case. The cases are genuinely interesting.

RATING: 5
[2016-04-22]
Egy London térkép, egy helyi újság és egy rövid történet így indul a nyomozás a Sherlock Holmes társasban. A játékosok Londonban próbálják együttesen vagy egymás ellen vetélkedve megfejteni az adott rejtélyt, jó esetben a nagy nevű detektívnél kevesebb utánajárással. A játékot úgy kell elképzelni, mint a régi Kaland Játék Kockázat könyveket. Minden lényeges helyszínhez tartozik egy rövid történetrész, amiből a fontos dolgokat leszűrve lehet közelebb jutni az ügy megoldásához. A KJK-val ellentétben itt nincs harc és kiesni sem lehet. Amikor a játékos úgy érzi mindent tud és megfejtette a rejtélyt, akkor a kaland végén található kérdésekre válaszolva pontozhatja a teljesítményét. A SHCD nem igazán társasjáték abban a tekintetben, hogy a játék szinte csak olvasásból áll. Akik szeretik a XIX. század Angliáját és történelmét, szeretik a dedukciót azok biztosan jól fognak szórakozni az alapjátékhoz tartozó 10 kaland során. A játék csak erős angoltudással játszható. Egy-egy kaland csak egyszer játszható. A játéknak a pechje esetemben, hogy a TIME Stories után próbáltam, így nekem annál kisebb élményt adott a puritánsága és behatárolt történelmi része miatt.

RATING: 8
Complexity: Medium
Players:  1-8 (probably best with 1-3)
Games Played: 10/10

I don't really know how to classify this game.  It's essentially ten Sherlock Holmes stories written in a choose-your-own-adventure format.  It's also really freaking hard.  Even without trying to beat Sherlock, you have to read carefully and piece the clues together.  If I have one complaint, it's that the game discourages exploration by penalizing you for taking too many steps, and as a consequence, it can be difficult to know when to stop exploring.  It is a terribly exhausting mental exercise but at the end of the day, it is quite a lot of fun.

RATING: 7
Commenting on this game after one or two plays is like commenting on a compilation of short stories after reading one.  It's more like a series of interactive stories than a game.  It's less a game to analyze than an activity to enjoy together.

We played our first case with another couple who owned the game.  Our hosts were on the third case, so we went with that one. We didn't actually solve the case, but I later discovered that it wasn't our fault. We were playing what is reputed to be the only "broken" case of the 10. In a way, it was a good one to play. My girlfriend and I hadn't played before, but she enjoyed the game very much. When I told her it was supposed to be the worst of the lot, her enthusiasm only increased. It was a very satisfying evening and prompted us to get a copy for ourselves.

My main complaint about the game is that there are a lot of typos and translation errors.  Many of them are so obvious that it's hard to imagine how they could have slipped through even a passing read somewhere in the editing process.  For such a text-heavy game, it's disappointing to realize that evidently no editing was done for both the initial reprint and the second printing.  Worse yet, some of the errors affect the solutions.  There's no excuse for that.  It's a significant drawback that merits mention, though it only moderately dampens my enthusiasm.

More serious than the typos, as annoying as they are, are the errors in the cases themselves.  Case #3 and #6 are broken in that the solutions don't make sense of the clues.  Players who might find this frustrating would do well to consult the forums on BGG before playing.  We avoided them out of fear of spoiling the cases.  In retrospect, we shouldn't have.  In particular, I highly recommend the excellent Irregular Handbook thread.

RATING: 6
You have to be very focused for a long time in this game.
You can't verify all the details, so you have to suggest, make hypothesis for the final solution. And even in the final solution, there will be some points unresolved (like in the real world). 
So, play with this on mind: don't try to resolve all the details, just focus on the essential things, and don't take too much time to stop and suggest a solution, or you will explore all the tracks and lose all the points to Sherlock Holmes, only to become frustrated in the end because not all the details have been resolved.

Other point: every case is "one shot", so you also are torn in half between exploring all the tracks or explore only the main paths but missing some clues...

RATING: 9.5
'Game' that tells you exiting stories of murder and mystery in sherlocks london. 

It's so engaging that if you don't end the case in the evening, it keeps haunting you the whole evening. 
Other cases keep you in amazement while you ponder over the evidence and testimonies that you gather as you go along. Even after the case has ended, you still keep thinking about it for a couple of days.

One thing we learned was to forget about focusing on beating Sherlock's score with minimum leads. It messes the game experience up. 

For one, you  would want to jump to early to the conclusion and therefore more chance to have the wrong answer. You blew the case and the case is still lost, since you now know the perpetrator. 

Secondly, you miss out on the interesting moments, and the additional story lines within the case. So take your time and enjoy it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Consulting Detective is like no game I've ever played. I'm not sure it's fair to call it a game at all. In many ways it's more like a "choose your own adventure" book; and if it succeeds, it's because it avoids the fatal flaw of most gamebooks -- that by practical necessity each node usually has no more than four edges.

In a choose your own adventure, if someone mentions a Mr. Smith, it'll tell you, "to speak to Mr. Smith, turn to page 5". In Consulting Detective, the paragraphs are entirely divorced from the navigation. You can find Mr. Smith, of course, but you'll have to look him up in the phone book. This is where it becomes more of a game and less of a book: if there are five Smiths and you choose wrong, you waste a unit of game-time, which allows your opponent(s) to get ahead -- the fastest to solve the crime wins.

There are also some clues in the day's newspaper or discoverable by examining the map.

I don't enjoy it as a competitive exercise, though, as it fails both mechanically (everyone has to share the paragraphs, everyone has to read the newspaper) or socially (everyone is silently reading the paragraphs, newspaper). As a cooperative or solo exercise, it's a lot of fun -- like being in a mystery novel. If you don't read closely and take notes, it's entirely possible to never solve the crime, and even if you do you sometimes have to backtrack after chasing a red herring.

Solving it [i]with[/i] someone, though -- maybe taking turns reading out paragraphs -- is just as enjoyable, and a fine way to spend a rainy afternoon.

RATING: 6

Even the map and Allies location codes are wrong. 

Great concept for a game and a unique experience to be sure, but structurally flawed in a few places - inexcusable for a release first published in 1981.

Works OK as a solitaire experience (just don't expect to beat Holmes).

RATING: 10
The following contains NO SPOILERS.

CASE 1: Got tricked. I was on the right track but then I found an alternative motive which somehow convinced me and I made a wrong bet. So close. Now I know to check all the leads before making any assumptions.
CASE 2: So, I was stupid. I got the killer and understood much else, even some of the little things, but the motive escaped me. Now, of course the solution was logical and the clues were plain to see but I still didn't get it. Frustrating and exhilarating at the same time. Can't wait for the next one. I made the decision to investigate every case thoroughly and not to compete with Holmes. Two reasons: Holmes can't be beat and the cases are so well written that I want to enjoy them fully.
CASE 3: A bit easier case, although part of the case was spoiled by a case of The Mansion Murders but maybe not by that much, the solution was quite plain to see. Solved the case and knew almost everything about it. 
CASE 4: Solved it. Good case, a bit complicated at first sight but not really after that. Only bits of the coded message went unsolved.
CASE 5: Solved, quite easy especially when compared to some of the other cases. 
CASE 6:Solved. 
CASE 7: The Thames Murders was one of the best cases of the Consulting Detective. Nicely complicated case which made me really scrap my head until I understood and admitted that it really had happened what I thought had happened. Solved completely.
CASE 8:Good case again. Quite simple especially when comparing it to the case that preceded it, but interesting nonetheless. Mostly solved, a small detail escaped me.
CASE 9: At beginning this was quite easy and I realized some basic details very soon. Best part of the case was how it surprised me with bitter tenderness. Very good case.
CASE 10: Like the previous, some details, even major, were easily grasped at the beginning of the case - but the rest of the details were very nicely done and made the case truly excellent. I really liked the motive, which, I must admit, espaced me.

Consulting Detective is definately one of the very best and without a doubt the most original game I have ever have an honour to play. Although all the cases weren't that deep or hard as one could wish, they were very immersive and very well written. I was glued to the game from the moment I started a new case and was thinking about clues even when I wasn't playing.

RATING: 7
It seems odd that this is considered a board game. The only mechanics are reading, discussing and note-taking. It is much more of a literary activity than a board game. 

In my first game I was a bit bored during the first hour or so (whilst being read aloud to with less than gripping material), but it got more enjoyable once we had a few leads and started putting clues together. In the end we got the right answers to all 8 questions, which was very gratifying. We only visited 4 more locations than Sherlock, so we got a decent score too.

I've played it a handful of times now and while it makes for a somewhat interesting activity, I don't come away feeling like I've played a board game, so it's a little unsatisfactory as a way to spend game night in that regard. I have to be in a certain mood to play this, would probably never suggest it myself, and have to accept that I just need to sit through the boring part to get to the good part. My primary gaming partner/opponent loves it though, so we're gradually working our way through the scenarios.

RATING: 9
This is a unique game.  As a Homicide Detective (in real life), I find that it deviates from reality in that real detectives follow-up on multiple paths of investigation while Sherlock Holmes does not. He'll have a hunch and follow only one path, avoiding divergent evidence paths and getting right to the point.Because you are racing against him, you are forced to do the same.  We have found that he occasionally presents findings that his case summary clue path (in reconstruction) never reveals.  With these snivellings aside, it's a rollicking trip through Victorian era London and the Holmes mythos.  I've enjoyed it a great deal and will regret when we inevitably run out of cases... there will be no replay value once all of the investigations have been conducted.

RATING: 10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Reading books is a wonderful but lonely business. Though you can discuss them with other readers, at the moment of their height, when the narrative fully engulfs you and mesmerises you, you are completely alone unable to share the feelings they create on you with anyone.

Enter Sherlock Holmes Consulting Detective, and you now have 10 detective stories that you can literally live with your friends/gaming group.
That is a remarkable goal for a boardgame, and with strong stories like Tin Soldier, it becomes a remarkable achievement.

The game is simple at its premise, yet undeniably efficient at what it does. By giving you complete freedom, it detaches you from the "board game" aspect of it and allows you to be fully drawn to its story. Just by reading the rules you may wonder, can't I just point out at a random place on the map and solve the case with pure luck? Sure you can but you can also abandon your work to try to win the lottery - does this make your work meaningless?

The overflow of information really creates a world that is begging for deduction, and the players will find themselves digging into fully detailed and amazing newspapers trying to find relevant information on their case, reading half readable personal notes in the writing style of the era, going through flavorful dialogs and smart interrogations again and again trying to deduce their meaning, debating on how to proceed next and forming theories on the solution of the case.

Overall all players are constantly enganged, and unless you have some detective genious inside your group that somehow solves anything himself you'll find that everyone involved will contribute his bit as the story moves on.

The goal of the game is to solve the case (correctly) as fast as you can and thus it really is a mental challenge. It makes your every move debated and meaningful. Sure you can roam Londom, visit 50 locations and obviously you'll have solved the case but the thing is you'll really feel good if you do it faster. Being half as good as Sherlock himself is something that is hard earned in this game, and therefore worth it. (unlike say, Time Stories which is much less challenging and more "live the experience" in its focus. Sherlock btw and imo, is better even in the "live the experience" aspect.)

Overall I loved Sherlock Holmes. It successfully blends the world of boardgames with the world of books, and it does so with 10 apparently great detective stories. Definitely an experience I recommend. Really looking forward to play more.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

The best, and in many regards only, mystery solving game. Story telling, role playing, brain taxing fun that exploits its components to the very limit. PLAY THIS GAME.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
[thing=2511][/thing] is, well, an unusual game.

First, the amount of rules: read the introduction of the case. Now, go wherever you want whenever you want (following the map by using the Directory, which contains the adresses of everyone of interest, and also the adress of several business locations) and talk (read) with the people there. Read the newspaper of the day, if pleases you. Also, you might want to talk with some fine folks that usually are aware of things (like Mycroft, journalists, go to the morgue or Scotland Yard, and such). Now, also whenever you want, you can return to present your fiddings regarding the case to Sherlock. The booklet will contain some questions, each will be worth an amount of points. Then, for every location you went above the number of those that Sherlock did, you will lose 5 points. Now, compare the amount you achieve with that of Holmes (he always gets 100 points). Then, you can feel good or bad, depending on how well you did. And that is it. Well, less than it, actually, since my last bit of feelings is more a thing I added. That is all the rules.

In every case you will have at your disposal: the booklet of the case (you can always consulte passages you read), the newspaper, the Directory (the adress of many things and persons in London) and the map of London, and this one, by the way, one of the players (we played in 4 people) thought it was utterly unnecessary, since how close one thing is to other, or the relation of one to another, wasn't playing any roll in the case. But then, in the end, the game hit us hard in the nutsack for not using it properly (you know, as a map). So great.

[thing=2511][/thing] is, probably, one of the most pure experiences of playing that one can have. There is no board (the map is not a board, you don't take action on it, you use it, as I pointed out, as a reference), no meeples, no pieces, no tokens, no money, no cards, no keeping rounds (though, you can see every new location as a new round), no gameplay time as we usually time it. What there are: reading and talking. After the reading of what happened in the case we started discussing: where to go? With whom to talk? What information we had? What means this or that? What about visiting the school for girls? And after we decided to go to a place, and the reading of what occurred there, we returned to the discussions. And now what? What we take it from this? Which lines/leads we now can follow? Usually quite a few, but it was worth our time to follow most of them? Which was more likely to actually advance our case? What was the place - or the person - with the best chances of providing clues? We compared notes, explored options, created scenarios - this because of this and this. Yes... but what about [i]this[/i]? Well, [i]this[/i] I can't explain properly right now. So, which place or person would shed light on this matter? I think this one. But wouldn't he just say what we already know? Then, we can take a chance here. Yes, it can work. And things went this way during the whole game. [thing=2511][/thing] is a game of pure deduction, taking notes, clashing options.

A play of [thing=2511][/thing] ends when the players want. You can visit 10 places, 50 or two. The decisions is yours, always. You know you are "running" against Holmes, but you did not know how many places/persons he went/talked to. I doubt that in any of the cases Sherlock needed to go to 50 places to solve the case, but the main question here is: which is worth more - beating Holmes or solving the case? For us it was solving the case, and only after we had a solid overall view of most of the things in the case, and a scenario that answered (for us) most of our own questions, that we went to see the end result. And this can't be taken lightly: once you read the solution of the case, you probably won't be able to ever playing it again since the challenge will be gone. There aren't any variations in the set up, nor event cards, nor different characters to take - the case will always be the same, the result will be the same, the clues will be in the same places.

So, yes, [thing=2511][/thing] has a "due date" - after the 10 cases, it is over. Except by reasons of really weak memory, disease and amnesia, even many years after playing you won't be able to fully enter a play of one of the cases (since, you might start not remembering things, but then something clicks, you remember, and now you basically can't participate). However, this is all that much important? There are lots of games I bought and probably won't clock 10 plays of them. But, more relevant, a game that gives you 10 memorable experiences? I can't, as up to right now, say that it will actually be 10 memorable experiences, but the first case was, and I doubt that the others will fall short.

The amount of work of the authors in the cases is awe inspiring - the newspaper and so many places with informations (most not relevant, but there, anyway). The only thing I wished it was present in the game was an internal clock - but I know this would make the game way more linear, so I'm fine without it. However, for those that want to enjoy something in the likes of this, you can download some of the Sherlock Holmes Solo Mysteries (a game book series) [url=http://www.abandonia.com/pt/gamebooks#Sherlock+Holmes+Solo+Mysteries]here[/url] - or go after the actual books (but they somewhat old).

It was a night of talks, exploring in our minds. We visited places, talked to people - and even going to only 9 different places, still the play lasted for almost four hours! The play reminded me of when, in college, we discussed books in the class of literary analysis - many views trying to reach an overall acceptance of the intent of the whole thing. 

Anyway, I'm totally excited to tackle the next mistery! Baker Street Irregulars to the work!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
SH Consulting Detective is an extremely hard game to rate. Each case has a certain flow of investigating, getting stuck, and rising excitement as you find the last piece of the puzzle.

But it all comes down to the ending. The game offers several leads that are always available, such as Scotland Yard, the criminology lab, or the morgue. But the list is comprised of some 15 leads, and it's almost never clear which one is going to have useful information. Sometimes the criminology lab has the deceased possessions, while other times Scotland Yard is holding them. Sometimes you'll need to consult with your criminal contact and he'll straight up give you the criminals name, while other times he'll have no clue about the criminal activities going on. In almost every case 1 or 2 of these contacts is essential to solving the case, while the other 13 or so are completely useless.

It's frustrating when Holmes jumps between these omnipresent leads, always knowing exactly which one will have the treasure trove of information needed to continue. Especially since, in general, none of the case specific leads will point you towards one of Holmes contacts. When you solve 75% of a case, but miss the final piece because you didn't bother talking to contact number 13, it feels unrewarding, like the game is cheating.

But some of the cases are really rewarding to play. The cases where the endings feel like a natural conclusion of the events of the case are the best, where if you solve the case it's because you used your deductive insight wisely, or if you lose it's because you missed an angle of the case, not because you missed a lead. If every case were like that this would score a 9 easily. Unfortunately, every case is written differently and not all of them are equal.

RATING: 9
Choose your own adventure for adults, with better writing. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Solve the mystery game, where you read various paragraphs in a large book, decide who to talk to or where to visit next, which gives a new paragraph to read. So, you build up the story in this way, and eventually, if you're attentive enough, you piece together the various items you've read to solve the mystery.

Lots of reading of course, but the fun part is deciding what to do next. "Let's go talk to the office manager", or "What's the coroner have to say about that?"

The only drawback is that with just 10 cases in the game, you're pretty much going to have to track down the expansions once you run through them.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
To be quite frank, this game just isn't for me. For the first case, we included the murderer on our initial shortlist of suspects, but we then ruled that person out because that person seemed way too obvious. (The person's motive was admittedly different from what we originally suspected; however, that didn't particularly matter to me.) In addition, there were at least two places where our group wanted to follow leads, but we weren't able to do so simply because we couldn't find the locations on the asininely small map. I feel like by the time a group becomes more familiar with the map, they will have gone through all the cases and that knowledge will be pointless. At this stage, I'm looking for games that are both entertaining and long-lasting. This game offers neither, nor does it have anything unique enough that I would be willing to overlook its flaws.

RATING: 7
One solo play to practice the game before trying with a group. Very promising game, more like an investigation than a game, really. Not easy by any means, and it is so well written, that you might be lured to just investigate more and more and not trying to solve quickly (the more you investigate, the less points you get).

There are 10 cases in the box, and you can play each case once, because they scripted - they are almost like novels, but you can choose the parts (clues) you are interested in most.

Second play with a group of 5, still the solution was a mystery us, but the it was fun. If I think as a game, this might not be very good, but as a mystery or riddle, this is great. Some hints are obvious, most are not.

Third play, solo this time. Now I got quite a good picture of the crime, but the final things were mysteries. Ended up investigating lots of unnecessary places in the end while looking for something useful. Best as a solo or with a small enthuastic group?

RATING: 8
A detective game where you try to solve a mystery together.
The game consists of 10  cases, a map of london, and a dictionary. You take turns choosing which lead to follow. To follow a lead, you read the matching paragraph in the casebook which gives you clues to the solution. When you feel you have solved the case you compare your solution with the Sherlock Holmes solution.
A choose your own detective story.

This one is unique, and not for everyone.  It is kind of like work, but in a cool way.  My wife and I play it together occasionally.  There is a group of fun little mysteries to solve.  It definitely has a finite shelf life though… once you finish the cases included in the game, there isn't much replay-ability, although I hear there are some fan-created cases.   

Update as of 6/15/17: We took a long time off from this game and decided to give it another go. I think I may not be one of the people this game is for. The feeling of work outweighs the feeling of fun or satisfaction for me. I don't even think it feels like a game. If I never played this game again, I'd be ok with that.

RATING: N/A
Story telling, murder mystery deduction game. I would also add role playing because the game materials immerse you in theme and I challenge you not to feel like a murder investigator. I loved detective stories growing up and I enjoy story telling games so I knew this would be a joy... and it is. Ten very well concocted scenarios, daily newspapers are a brilliant touch, a map of London, and a directory of suspects. And with most story telling games, the game lasts for as long as you want it to.  Thank God that they give you Sherlock's break down of each crime so that you can see all the stuff you missed.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
One of the most unique gaming experiences I've ever had.  It's basically an interactive crime/mystery novel, with you as the PI.  You decide who to investigate, you decide where to investigate, and you put the pieces together. I do wish the map was a bit more durable feeling, maybe printed on cardstock instead of poster paper, but quite honestly we haven't used it much anyway.  The quiz format is pretty open ended, which is good, because then you can't be right just by virtue of guessing the correct suspect from a list of multiple choice answers. This does lead to some ambiguity in the scoring, but we'll probably ignore the scoring going forward anyway. It's just not that important in the grand scheme of the awesome experience that is this game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
In a similar vein to TIME Stories the scoring rules incentivise the player not to explore the case to the fullest in favour of an Occam's Razor, Holmesian approach. Use the scoring rules if you have any interest in the competitive aspect or if you want some pressure to finish the game within a reasonable time frame. If you have all day or you are happy with splitting the case across multiple plays then ignore scoring and absorb the cases to their fullest. Given the "one-shot" nature of the cases you'll get more value for money ignoring the scoring.

< September 2016
> UKGE 2017
